width="120"/></a>\n<div class="" style="width:100%; text-align:center; border-top:1px #aaa solid"><i><a href="/wiki/Pioneer_program" title="Pioneer program">Pioneer</a></i></div><div style="width:100%"><div class="noprint" style="float: left; text-align:left;padding-right:0.5em;">&#8592;&#160;<a href="/wiki/Pioneer_10" title="Pioneer 10">Pioneer 10</a></div><div class="noprint" style="float: right; text-align:right;padding-left:0.5em;"><a href="/wiki/Pioneer_Venus_Orbiter" title="Pioneer Venus Orbiter">Pioneer 12</a>&#160;&#8594;</div></div>&#160;</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><i><b>Pioneer 11</b></i> (also known as <i><b>Pioneer G</b></i>) is a 259-kilogram (571&#160;lb) <a href="/wiki/Robotic_spacecraft" title="Robotic spacecraft">robotic</a> <a href="/wiki/Space_probe" title="Space probe">space probe</a> launched by <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a> on April 6, 1973 to study the <a href="/wiki/Asteroid_belt" title="Asteroid belt">asteroid belt</a>, the environment around <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a> and <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">Saturn</a>, <a href="/wiki/Solar_wind" title="Solar wind">solar wind</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_ray" title="Cosmic ray">cosmic rays</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PionOdys_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-PionOdys-1">[1]</a></sup> It was the first probe to <a href="/wiki/Exploration_of_Saturn" title="Exploration of Saturn">encounter Saturn</a> and the second to fly through the <a href="/wiki/Asteroid_belt#Exploration" title="Asteroid belt">asteroid belt</a> and <a href="/wiki/Exploration_of_Jupiter" title="Exploration of Jupiter">by Jupiter</a>. Thereafter, <i>Pioneer 11</i> became the <a href="/wiki/List_of_artificial_objects_leaving_the_Solar_System" title="List of artificial objects leaving the Solar System">second of five artificial objects</a> to achieve the <a href="/wiki/Escape_velocity" title="Escape velocity">escape velocity</a> that will allow them to <a href="/wiki/Solar_System#Farthest_regions" title="Solar System">leave the Solar System</a>. Due to power constraints and the vast distance to the probe, the last routine contact with the spacecraft was on September 30, 1995, and the last good engineering data was received on November 24, 1995.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-InDepth_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-InDepth-3">[3]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="toc" id="toc"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2>Contents</h2><span tocsection-22"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Mission_background">Mission background</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pioneer_11&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Mission background">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pioneer_11&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Approved in February 1969, <i>Pioneer 11</i> and its twin probe, <i><a href="/wiki/Pioneer_10" title="Pioneer 10">Pioneer 10</a></i>, were the first to be designed for exploring the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Outer_Solar_System" title="Outer Solar System">outer Solar System</a>. Yielding to multiple proposals throughout the 1960s, early mission objectives were defined as:\n</p>\n<ul><li>Explore the <a href="/wiki/Interplanetary_medium" title="Interplanetary medium">interplanetary medium</a> beyond the orbit of Mars</li>\n<li>Investigate the nature of the asteroid belt from the scientific standpoint and assess the belt\'s possible hazard to missions to the outer planets.</li>\n<li>Explore the <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Jupiter" title="Atmosphere of Jupiter">environment of Jupiter</a>.</li></ul>\n<p>Subsequent planning for an encounter with Saturn added many more goals:\n</p>\n<ul><li>Map the magnetic field of Saturn and determine its intensity, direction, and structure.</li>\n<li>Determine how many electrons and protons of various energies are distributed along the trajectory of the spacecraft through the Saturn system.</li>\n<li>Map the interaction of the Saturn system with the <a href="/wiki/Solar_wind" title="Solar wind">solar wind</a>.</li>\n<li>Measure the temperature of Saturn\'s atmosphere and that of Titan, the largest satellite of Saturn.</li>\n<li>Determine the structure of the upper atmosphere of Saturn where molecules are expected to be electrically charged and form an ionosphere.</li>\n<li>Map the thermal structure of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Saturn" title="Atmosphere of Saturn">Saturn\'s atmosphere</a> by infrared observations coupled with <a href="/wiki/Radio_occultation" title="Radio occultation">radio occultation</a> data.</li>\n<li>Obtain spin-scan images of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saturnian_system" title="Saturnian system">Saturnian system</a> in two colors during the encounter sequence and polarimetry measurements of the planet.</li>\n<li>Probe the ring system and the atmosphere of Saturn with S-band radio occultation.</li>\n<li>Determine more precisely the masses of Saturn and its larger satellites by accurate observations of the effects of their gravitational fields on the motion of the spacecraft.</li>\n<li>As a precursor to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Voyager_Program" title="Voyager Program">Mariner Jupiter/Saturn mission</a>, verify the environment of the ring plane to find out where it may be safely crossed by the Mariner spacecraft without serious damage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup></li></ul>\n<p><i>Pioneer 11</i> was built by <a href="/wiki/TRW_Inc." title="TRW Inc.">TRW</a> and managed as part of the <a href="/wiki/Pioneer_program" title="Pioneer program">Pioneer program</a> by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NASA_Ames_Research_Center" title="NASA Ames Research Center">NASA Ames Research Center</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> A backup unit, <a href="/wiki/Pioneer_H" title="Pioneer H">Pioneer H</a>, is currently on display in the "Milestones of Flight" exhibit at the <a href="/wiki/National_Air_and_Space_Museum" title="National Air and Space Museum">National Air and Space Museum</a> in <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">Washington, D.C.</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> Many elements of the mission proved to be critical in the planning of the <a href="/wiki/Voyager_program" title="Voyager program"><i>Voyager</i> program</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-burrows_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-burrows-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>266&#8211;8</span></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Spacecraft_design">Spacecraft design</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pioneer_11&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Spacecraft design">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The <i>Pioneer 11</i> bus measured 36 centimeters (14&#160;in) deep and with six 76-centimeter-long (30&#160;in) panels forming the hexagonal structure. The bus housed propellant to control the orientation of the probe and eight of the twelve scientific instruments. The spacecraft had a mass of 260 kilograms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PionOdys_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-PionOdys-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>42</span></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Attitude_control_and_propulsion">Attitude control and propulsion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pioneer_11&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Attitude control and propulsion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="floatright">\n<div style="width: 75px; height: 75px; overflow: hidden;">\n<div style="position: relative; top: -15px; left: -0px; width: 339px"><div class="noresize"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pioneer_10_-_Pioneer_11_-_thrusters_-_p43.jpg"><img alt="Pioneer 10 - Pioneer 11 - thrusters - p43.jpg" data-file-height="159" data-file-width="717" decoding="async" height="75" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Pioneer_10_-_Pioneer_11_-_thrusters_-_p43.jpg/339px-Pioneer_10_-_Pioneer_11_-_thrusters_-_p43.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Pioneer_10_-_Pioneer_11_-_thrusters_-_p43.jpg/509px-Pioneer_10_-_Pioneer_11_-_thrusters_-_p43.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Pioneer_10_-_Pioneer_11_-_thrusters_-_p43.jpg/678px-Pioneer_10_-_Pioneer_11_-_thrusters_-_p43.jpg 2x" width="339"/></a></div></div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<dl><dd>Orientation of the spacecraft was maintained with six 4.5-<a href="/wiki/Newton_(unit)" title="Newton (unit)">N</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Astronautix_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Astronautix-8">[8]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hydrazine" title="Hydrazine">hydrazine</a> <a href="/wiki/Monopropellant_rocket" title="Monopropellant rocket">monopropellant</a> thrusters: pair one maintained a constant spin-rate of 4.8 <a href="/wiki/Revolutions_per_minute" title="Revolutions per minute">rpm</a>, pair two controlled the forward thrust, pair three controlled attitude. Information for the orientation was provided by performing <a href="/wiki/Conical_scanning" title="Conical scanning">conical scanning</a> maneuvers to track Earth in its orbit,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> a <a href="/wiki/Star_tracker#Star_tracker" title="Star tracker">star sensor</a> able to reference <a href="/wiki/Canopus" title="Canopus">Canopus</a>, and two <a href="/wiki/Sun_sensor" title="Sun sensor">Sun sensors</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PionOdys_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-PionOdys-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>42&#8211;43</span></sup></dd></dl>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Communications">Communications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pioneer_11&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Communications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<dl><dd>The space probe included a redundant system  <a href="/wiki/Transceiver" title="Transceiver">transceivers</a>, one attached to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High-gain_antenna" title="High-gain antenna">high-gain antenna</a>, the other to an omni-antenna and medium-gain antenna. Each transceiver was 8 watts and transmitted data across the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/S-band" title="S-band">S-band</a> using 2110 MHz for the uplink from Earth and 2292 MHz for the downlink to Earth with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deep_Space_Network" title="Deep Space Network">Deep Space Network</a> tracking the signal. Prior to transmitting data, the probe used a <a href="/wiki/Convolutional_code" title="Convolutional code">convolutional encoder</a> to allow <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Error_correction" title="Error correction">correction of errors</a> in the received data on Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PionOdys_1-3"><a href="#cite_note-PionOdys-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>43</span></sup></dd></dl>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Power">Power</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pioneer_11&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Power">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="floatright"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Two_of_four_SNAP_19_RTGs_of_a_Pioneer_space_probe.jpg" title="SNAP-19 RTG"><img alt="SNAP-19 RTG" data-file-height="197" data-file-width="233" decoding="async" height="63" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Two_of_four_SNAP_19_RTGs_of_a_Pioneer_space_probe.jpg/75px-Two_of_four_SNAP_19_RTGs_of_a_Pioneer_space_probe.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Two_of_four_SNAP_19_RTGs_of_a_Pioneer_space_probe.jpg/113px-Two_of_four_SNAP_19_RTGs_of_a_Pioneer_space_probe.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Two_of_four_SNAP_19_RTGs_of_a_Pioneer_space_probe.jpg/150px-Two_of_four_SNAP_19_RTGs_of_a_Pioneer_space_probe.jpg 2x" width="75"/></a></div>\n<dl><dd><i>Pioneer 11</i> used four SNAP-19 <a href="/wiki/Radioisotope_thermoelectric_generator" title="Radioisotope thermoelectric generator">radioisotope thermoelectric generators</a> (RTGs) (<i><b><a href="/wiki/File:Cutdrawing_of_an_SNAP_19_RTG.jpg" title="File:Cutdrawing of an SNAP 19 RTG.jpg">see diagram</a></b></i>). They were positioned on two three-rod trusses, each 3 meters (9&#160;feet 10&#160;inches) in length and 120 degrees apart. This was expected to be a safe distance from the sensitive scientific experiments carried on board. Combined, the RTGs provided 155 watts at launch, and decayed to 140 W in transit to Jupiter. The spacecraft required 100 W to power all systems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PionOdys_1-4"><a href="#cite_note-PionOdys-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>44&#8211;45</span></sup></dd></dl>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Computer">Computer</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pioneer_11&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Computer">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<dl><dd>Much of the computation for the mission was performed on Earth and transmitted to the probe, where it was able to retain in memory, up to five commands of the 222 possible entries by ground controllers. The spacecraft included two command decoders and a command distribution unit, a very limited form of processor, to direct operations on the spacecraft. This system required that mission operators prepare commands long in advance of transmitting them to the probe. A data storage unit was included to record up to 6,144 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bytes" title="Bytes">bytes</a> of information gathered by the instruments. The digital telemetry unit would then be used to prepare the collected data in one of the thirteen possible formats before transmitting it back to Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PionOdys_1-5"><a href="#cite_note-PionOdys-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>38</span></sup></dd></dl>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Scientific_instruments">Scientific instruments</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pioneer_11&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Scientific instruments">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Pioneer had one additional instrument more than Pioneer 10, a flux-gate magnetometer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable">\n<tbody><tr>\n<th colspan="2" style="text-align: left">Helium Vector <a href="/wiki/Magnetometer" title="Magnetometer">Magnetometer</a> (<b>HVM</b>)\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pioneer_10-11_-_P50_-_fx.jpg"><img alt="Pioneer 10-11 - P50 - fx.jpg" data-file-height="710" data-file-width="944" decoding="async" height="113" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Pioneer_10-11_-_P50_-_fx.jpg/150px-Pioneer_10-11_-_P50_-_fx.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Pioneer_10-11_-_P50_-_fx.jpg/225px-Pioneer_10-11_-_P50_-_fx.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Pioneer_10-11_-_P50_-_fx.jpg/300px-Pioneer_10-11_-_P50_-_fx.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a>\n</td>\n<td>Measured the fine structure of the interplanetary magnetic field, mapped the Jovian magnetic field, and provided magnetic field measurements to evaluate solar wind interaction with Jupiter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NSSDCHVM_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-NSSDCHVM-11">[11]</a></sup>\n<ul><li><span style="font-size:85%;"><b>Principal investigator:</b> Edward Smith / JPL</span></li>\n<li><span style="font-size:85%;"><b>Data:</b> <a class="external text" href="http://pds-ppi.igpp.ucla.edu/search/?filter=PN_5001,PN_6001&amp;title=Pioneer_11_Helium_Vector_Magnetometer" rel="nofollow">PDS/PPI data catalog</a>, <a class="external text" href="ftp://nssdcftp.gsfc.nasa.gov/spacecraft_data/pioneer/pioneer11/mag/" rel="nofollow">NSSDC data archive</a></span></li></ul>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th colspan="2" style="text-align: left">Quadrispherical <a href="/wiki/Plasma_(physics)" title="Plasma (physics)">Plasma</a> Analyzer\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pioneer_10-11_-_P51b_-_fx.jpg"><img alt="Pioneer 10-11 - P51b - fx.jpg" data-file-height="742" data-file-width="965" decoding="async" height="115" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Pioneer_10-11_-_P51b_-_fx.jpg/150px-Pioneer_10-11_-_P51b_-_fx.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Pioneer_10-11_-_P51b_-_fx.jpg/225px-Pioneer_10-11_-_P51b_-_fx.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Pioneer_10-11_-_P51b_-_fx.jpg/300px-Pioneer_10-11_-_P51b_-_fx.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a>\n</td>\n<td>Peered through a hole in the large dish-shaped antenna to detect particles of the solar wind originating from the Sun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NSSDCQPA_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-NSSDCQPA-12">[12]</a></sup>\n<ul><li><span style="font-size:85%;"><b>Principal investigator:</b> Aaron Barnes / NASA Ames Research Center (<a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20000520044455/http://arwen.arc.nasa.gov/" rel="nofollow">archived website</a>)</span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESimpson2001146_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESimpson2001146-13">[13]</a></sup></li>\n<li><span style="font-size:85%;"><b>Data:</b> <a class="external text" href="http://pds-ppi.igpp.ucla.edu/search/?filter=PN_5001,PN_6001&amp;title=Pioneer_11_Quadrispherical_Plasma_Analyser" rel="nofollow">PDS/PPI data catalog</a>, <a class="external text" href="ftp://nssdcftp.gsfc.nasa.gov/spacecraft_data/pioneer/pioneer11/plasma/" rel="nofollow">NSSDC data archive</a></span></li></ul>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th colspan="2" style="text-align: left"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Charged_Particle" title="Charged Particle">Charged Particle</a> Instrument (<b>CPI</b>)\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pioneer_10-11_-_P52a_-_fx.jpg"><img alt="Pioneer 10-11 - P52a - fx.jpg" data-file-height="716" data-file-width="970" decoding="async" height="111" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Pioneer_10-11_-_P52a_-_fx.jpg/150px-Pioneer_10-11_-_P52a_-_fx.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Pioneer_10-11_-_P52a_-_fx.jpg/225px-Pioneer_10-11_-_P52a_-_fx.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Pioneer_10-11_-_P52a_-_fx.jpg/300px-Pioneer_10-11_-_P52a_-_fx.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a>\n</td>\n<td>Detected cosmic rays in the Solar System.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NSSDCCPI_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-NSSDCCPI-14">[14]</a></sup>\n<ul><li><span style="font-size:85%;"><b>Principal investigator:</b> <a href="/wiki/John_Alexander_Simpson" title="John Alexander Simpson">John Simpson</a> / University of Chicago</span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESimpson2001146_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESimpson2001146-13">[13]</a></sup></li>\n<li><span style="font-size:85%;"><b>Data:</b> <a class="external text" href="ftp://nssdcftp.gsfc.nasa.gov/spacecraft_data/pioneer/pioneer11/particle/cpi/" rel="nofollow">NSSDC data archive</a></span></li></ul>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th colspan="2" style="text-align: left"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cosmic_Ray" title="Cosmic Ray">Cosmic Ray</a> Telescope (<b>CRT</b>)\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pioneer_10-11_-_P52b_-_fx.jpg"><img alt="Pioneer 10-11 - P52b - fx.jpg" data-file-height="778" data-file-width="970" decoding="async" height="120" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Pioneer_10-11_-_P52b_-_fx.jpg/150px-Pioneer_10-11_-_P52b_-_fx.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Pioneer_10-11_-_P52b_-_fx.jpg/225px-Pioneer_10-11_-_P52b_-_fx.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Pioneer_10-11_-_P52b_-_fx.jpg/300px-Pioneer_10-11_-_P52b_-_fx.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a>\n</td>\n<td>Collected data on the composition of the cosmic ray particles and their energy ranges.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NSSDCCRT_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-NSSDCCRT-15">[15]</a></sup>\n<ul><li><span style="font-size:85%;"><b>Principal investigator:</b> <a href="/wiki/Frank_B._McDonald" title="Frank B. McDonald">Frank B. McDonald</a> / NASA Goddard Space Flight Center</span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESimpson2001146_13-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESimpson2001146-13">[13]</a></sup></li>\n<li><span style="font-size:85%;"><b>Data:</b> <a class="external text" href="http://pds-ppi.igpp.ucla.edu/search/?filter=PN_5001,PN_6001&amp;title=Pioneer_11_Cosmic_Ray_Telescope" rel="nofollow">PDS/PPI data catalog</a>, <a class="external text" href="ftp://nssdcftp.gsfc.nasa.gov/spacecraft_data/pioneer/pioneer11/particle/crt/" rel="nofollow">NSSDC data archive</a></span></li></ul>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th colspan="2" style="text-align: left"><a href="/wiki/Geiger_tube_telescope" title="Geiger tube telescope">Geiger Tube Telescope</a> (<b>GTT</b>)\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><div class="center" style="width:auto; margin-left:auto; margin-right:auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pioneer_10-11_-_p53_-_fx.jpg"><img alt="Pioneer 10-11 - p53 - fx.jpg" data-file-height="861" data-file-width="662" decoding="async" height="100" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Pioneer_10-11_-_p53_-_fx.jpg/77px-Pioneer_10-11_-_p53_-_fx.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Pioneer_10-11_-_p53_-_fx.jpg/115px-Pioneer_10-11_-_p53_-_fx.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Pioneer_10-11_-_p53_-_fx.jpg/154px-Pioneer_10-11_-_p53_-_fx.jpg 2x" width="77"/></a></div>\n</td>\n<td>Surveyed the intensities, energy spectra, and angular distributions of electrons and protons along the spacecraft\'s path through the radiation belts of Jupiter and Saturn.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NSSDCGTT_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-NSSDCGTT-16">[16]</a></sup>\n<ul><li><span style="font-size:85%;"><b>Principal investigator:</b> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/James_A._Van_Allen" title="James A. Van Allen">James A. Van Allen</a> / University of Iowa (<a class="external text" href="http://www-pw.physics.uiowa.edu/pioneer/home.html" rel="nofollow">website</a>)</span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESimpson2001146_13-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESimpson2001146-13">[13]</a></sup></li>\n<li><span style="font-size:85%;"><b>Data:</b> <a class="external text" href="http://pds-ppi.igpp.ucla.edu/search/?filter=PN_5001,PN_6001&amp;title=Pioneer_11_Gieger_Tube_Telescope" rel="nofollow">PDS/PPI data catalog</a>, <a class="external text" href="ftp://nssdcftp.gsfc.nasa.gov/spacecraft_data/pioneer/pioneer11/particle/gtt/" rel="nofollow">NSSDC data archive</a>, <a class="external text" href="ftp://nssdcftp.gsfc.nasa.gov/spacecraft_data/pioneer/pioneer11/geiger_tube_telescope_%28gtt%29/jupiter_encounter_data/" rel="nofollow">NSSDC Jupiter data archive</a></span></li></ul>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th colspan="2" style="text-align: left">Trapped <a href="/wiki/Radiation" title="Radiation">Radiation</a> Detector (<b>TRD</b>)\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pioneer_10-11_-_P54_-_fx.jpg"><img alt="Pioneer 10-11 - P54 - fx.jpg" data-file-height="667" data-file-width="949" decoding="async" height="105" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Pioneer_10-11_-_P54_-_fx.jpg/150px-Pioneer_10-11_-_P54_-_fx.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Pioneer_10-11_-_P54_-_fx.jpg/225px-Pioneer_10-11_-_P54_-_fx.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Pioneer_10-11_-_P54_-_fx.jpg/300px-Pioneer_10-11_-_P54_-_fx.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a>\n</td>\n<td>\n<p>Included an <i>unfocused <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cerenkov_counter" title="Cerenkov counter">Cerenkov counter</a></i> that detected the light emitted in a particular direction as particles passed through it recording electrons of energy, 0.5 to 12 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electron_volt" title="Electron volt">MeV</a>, an <i>electron scatter detector</i> for electrons of energy, 100 to 400 keV, and a <i>minimum ionizing detector</i> consisting of a solid-state diode that measured minimum ionizing particles (&lt;3 MeV)  and protons in the range of 50 to 350 MeV.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NSSDCTRD_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-NSSDCTRD-17">[17]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul><li><span style="font-size:85%;"><b>Principal investigator:</b> R. Fillius / University of California San Diego</span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESimpson2001146_13-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESimpson2001146-13">[13]</a></sup></li>\n<li><span style="font-size:85%;"><b>Data:</b> <a class="external text" href="ftp://nssdcftp.gsfc.nasa.gov/spacecraft_data/pioneer/pioneer11/particle/trd/" rel="nofollow">NSSDC hourly data archive</a>, <a class="external text" href="ftp://nssdcftp.gsfc.nasa.gov/spacecraft_data/pioneer/pioneer11/jovian_trapped_radiation/saturn_enctr_binary_reduction_dat/" rel="nofollow">NSSDC Saturn data archive</a></span></li></ul>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th colspan="2" style="text-align: left"><a href="/wiki/Meteoroid" title="Meteoroid">Meteoroid</a> Detectors\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pioneer_10-11_-_P56_-_fx.jpg"><img alt="Pioneer 10-11 - P56 - fx.jpg" data-file-height="549" data-file-width="950" decoding="async" height="87" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Pioneer_10-11_-_P56_-_fx.jpg/150px-Pioneer_10-11_-_P56_-_fx.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Pioneer_10-11_-_P56_-_fx.jpg/225px-Pioneer_10-11_-_P56_-_fx.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Pioneer_10-11_-_P56_-_fx.jpg/300px-Pioneer_10-11_-_P56_-_fx.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a>\n</td>\n<td>Twelve panels of pressurized cell detectors mounted on the back of the main dish antenna recorded penetrating impacts of small meteoroids.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NSSDCMD_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-NSSDCMD-18">[18]</a></sup>\n<ul><li><span style="font-size:85%;"><b>Principal investigator:</b> William Kinard / NASA Langley Research Center</span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESimpson2001146_13-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESimpson2001146-13">[13]</a></sup></li>\n<li><span style="font-size:85%;"><b>Data:</b> <a class="external text" href="http://nssdc.gsfc.nasa.gov/nmc/datasetSearch.do?spacecraft=Pioneer%2011&amp;experiment=Meteoroid%20Detectors" rel="nofollow">NSSDC data archive list</a></span></li></ul>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th colspan="2" style="text-align: left">Asteroid/Meteoroid Detector (<b>AMD</b>)\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pioneer_10-11_-_P55b_-_fx.jpg"><img alt="Pioneer 10-11 - P55b - fx.jpg" data-file-height="868" data-file-width="797" decoding="async" height="163" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Pioneer_10-11_-_P55b_-_fx.jpg/150px-Pioneer_10-11_-_P55b_-_fx.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Pioneer_10-11_-_P55b_-_fx.jpg/225px-Pioneer_10-11_-_P55b_-_fx.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Pioneer_10-11_-_P55b_-_fx.jpg/300px-Pioneer_10-11_-_P55b_-_fx.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a>\n</td>\n<td>Meteoroid-asteroid detector looked into space with four non-imaging telescopes to track particles ranging from close-by bits of dust to distant large asteroids.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NSSDCAMD_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-NSSDCAMD-19">[19]</a></sup>\n<ul><li><span style="font-size:85%;"><b>Principal investigator:</b> Robert Soberman / General Electric Company</span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESimpson2001146_13-6"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESimpson2001146-13">[13]</a></sup></li>\n<li><span style="font-size:85%;"><b>Data:</b> <a class="external text" href="http://nssdc.gsfc.nasa.gov/nmc/datasetSearch.do?spacecraft=Pioneer%2011&amp;experiment=Asteroid/Meteoroid%20Astronomy" rel="nofollow">NSSDC data archive list</a></span></li></ul>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th colspan="2" style="text-align: left">Ultraviolet <a href="/wiki/Photometer" title="Photometer">Photometer</a>\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pioneer_10-11_-_P57a_-_fx.jpg"><img alt="Pioneer 10-11 - P57a - fx.jpg" data-file-height="719" data-file-width="962" decoding="async" height="112" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Pioneer_10-11_-_P57a_-_fx.jpg/150px-Pioneer_10-11_-_P57a_-_fx.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Pioneer_10-11_-_P57a_-_fx.jpg/225px-Pioneer_10-11_-_P57a_-_fx.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Pioneer_10-11_-_P57a_-_fx.jpg/300px-Pioneer_10-11_-_P57a_-_fx.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a>\n</td>\n<td>Ultraviolet light was sensed to determine the quantities of hydrogen and helium in space and on Jupiter and Saturn.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NSSDCUVP_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-NSSDCUVP-20">[20]</a></sup>\n<ul><li><span style="font-size:85%;"><b>Principal investigator:</b> Darrell Judge / University of Southern California</span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESimpson2001146_13-7"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESimpson2001146-13">[13]</a></sup></li>\n<li><span style="font-size:85%;"><b>Data:</b> <a class="external text" href="http://ppi.pds.nasa.gov/search?filter=PN_5001,PN_6001&amp;title=Pioneer_11_Ultraviolet_Photometer" rel="nofollow">PDS/PPI data catalog</a>, <a class="external text" href="ftp://nssdcftp.gsfc.nasa.gov/spacecraft_data/pioneer/pioneer11/uv/" rel="nofollow">NSSDC data archive</a></span></li></ul>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th colspan="2" style="text-align: left">Imaging <a href="/wiki/Polarimeter" title="Polarimeter">Photopolarimeter</a> (<b>IPP</b>)\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pioneer_10-11_-_P60_-_fx.jpg"><img alt="Pioneer 10-11 - P60 - fx.jpg" data-file-height="723" data-file-width="954" decoding="async" height="114" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Pioneer_10-11_-_P60_-_fx.jpg/150px-Pioneer_10-11_-_P60_-_fx.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Pioneer_10-11_-_P60_-_fx.jpg/225px-Pioneer_10-11_-_P60_-_fx.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Pioneer_10-11_-_P60_-_fx.jpg/300px-Pioneer_10-11_-_P60_-_fx.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a>\n</td>\n<td>The imaging experiment relied upon the spin of the spacecraft to sweep a small telescope across the planet in narrow strips only 0.03 degrees wide, looking at the planet in red and blue light. These strips were then processed to build up a visual image of the planet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NSSDCIPP_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-NSSDCIPP-21">[21]</a></sup>\n<ul><li><span style="font-size:85%;"><b>Principal investigator:</b> <a href="/wiki/Tom_Gehrels" title="Tom Gehrels">Tom Gehrels</a> / University of Arizona</span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESimpson2001146_13-8"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESimpson2001146-13">[13]</a></sup></li>\n<li><span style="font-size:85%;"><b>Data:</b> <a class="external text" href="http://nssdc.gsfc.nasa.gov/nmc/datasetSearch.do?spacecraft=Pioneer%2011&amp;experiment=Imaging%20Photopolarimeter%20%28IPP%29" rel="nofollow">NSSDC data archive list</a></span></li></ul>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th colspan="2" style="text-align: left">Infrared <a href="/wiki/Radiometer" title="Radiometer">Radiometer</a>\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:P58_-_fx.jpg"><img alt="P58 - fx.jpg" data-file-height="748" data-file-width="956" decoding="async" height="117" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/P58_-_fx.jpg/150px-P58_-_fx.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/P58_-_fx.jpg/225px-P58_-_fx.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/P58_-_fx.jpg/300px-P58_-_fx.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a>\n</td>\n<td>Provided information on cloud temperature and the output of heat from Jupiter and Saturn.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NSSDCIRR_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-NSSDCIRR-22">[22]</a></sup>\n<ul><li><span style="font-size:85%;"><b>Principal investigator:</b> Andrew Ingersoll / California Institute of Technology</span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESimpson2001146_13-9"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESimpson2001146-13">[13]</a></sup></li></ul>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th colspan="2" style="text-align: left">Triaxial Fluxgate <a href="/wiki/Magnetometer" title="Magnetometer">Magnetometer</a>\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Triaxial_Fluxgate_Mars_Global_Surveyor_Magnetometer.gif"><img alt="Triaxial Fluxgate Mars Global Surveyor Magnetometer.gif" data-file-height="590" data-file-width="654" decoding="async" height="135" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Triaxial_Fluxgate_Mars_Global_Surveyor_Magnetometer.gif/150px-Triaxial_Fluxgate_Mars_Global_Surveyor_Magnetometer.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Triaxial_Fluxgate_Mars_Global_Surveyor_Magnetometer.gif/225px-Triaxial_Fluxgate_Mars_Global_Surveyor_Magnetometer.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Triaxial_Fluxgate_Mars_Global_Surveyor_Magnetometer.gif/300px-Triaxial_Fluxgate_Mars_Global_Surveyor_Magnetometer.gif 2x" width="150"/></a>\n</td>\n<td>Measured the magnetic fields of both Jupiter and Saturn. This instrument was not carried on <i>Pioneer 10</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NSSDCJMF_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-NSSDCJMF-23">[23]</a></sup>\n<ul><li><span style="width: 5%;"><div class="center" style="width:auto; margin-left:auto; margin-right:auto;">1985-02-</div>\n</td>\n<td scope="col" style="width: 95%px;">Instrument power sharing began due to declining generator power output.\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><div class="center" style="width:auto; margin-left:auto; margin-right:auto;">1990-</div>\n</td>\n<td>Passed the orbit of <a href="/wiki/Pluto" Pioneer 11 is 6.5 billion km from Earth.\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><div class="center" style="width:auto; margin-left:auto; margin-right:auto;">1995-11-24</div>\n</td>\n<td>Last signal received.\n</td></tr></tbody></table><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PionOdys_1-6"><a href="#cite_note-PionOdys-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>61&#8211;94</span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PDSOverview_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-PDSOverview-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DMuller_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-DMuller-25">[25]</a></sup>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Launch_and_trajectory">Launch and trajectory</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pioneer_11&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Launch and trajectory">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The <i>Pioneer 11</i> probe was launched on April 6, 1973 at 02:11:00 UTC, by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_Aeronautics_and_Space_Administration" title="National Aeronautics and Space Administration">National Aeronautics and Space Administration</a> from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cape_Canaveral_Air_Force_Station_Space_Launch_Complex_36" title="Cape Canaveral Air Force Station Space Launch Complex 36">Space Launch Complex 36A</a> at <a href="/wiki/Cape_Canaveral_Air_Force_Station" title="Cape Canaveral Air Force Station">Cape Canaveral</a>, Florida aboard an <a href="/wiki/Atlas-Centaur" title="Atlas-Centaur">Atlas-Centaur</a> launch vehicle. Its twin probe, <i><a href="/wiki/Pioneer_10" title="Pioneer 10">Pioneer 10</a></i>, had launched a year earlier on March 3, 1972. <i>Pioneer 11</i> was launched on a trajectory directly aimed at Jupiter without any prior gravitational assists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> In May 1974, Pioneer was retargeted to fly past Jupiter on a north-south trajectory enabling a Saturn flyby in 1979. The maneuver used 17 pounds of propellant, lasted 42 minutes and 36 seconds and increased Pioneer 11\'s speed by 230&#160;km/h.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> It also made two mid-course corrections, on April 11, 1973 and November 7, 1974.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-InDepth_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-InDepth-3">[3]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Encounter_with_Jupiter">Encounter with Jupiter</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pioneer_11&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Encounter with Jupiter">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Exploration_of_Jupiter" title="Exploration of Jupiter">Exploration of Jupiter</a></div>\n<p><i>Pioneer 11</i> flew past Jupiter in November and December 1974. During its closest approach, on December 2, it passed 42,828 kilometers (26,612&#160;mi) above the cloud tops.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PDSOverview_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-PDSOverview-24">[24]</a></sup> The probe obtained detailed images of the <a href="/wiki/Great_Red_Spot" title="Great Red Spot">Great Red Spot</a>, transmitted the first images of the immense polar regions, and determined the mass of Jupiter\'s moon <a href="/wiki/Callisto_(moon)" title="Callisto (moon)">Callisto</a>. Using the gravitational pull of Jupiter, a <a href="/wiki/Gravity_assist" title="Gravity assist">gravity assist</a> was used to alter the trajectory of the probe towards Saturn. On April 16, 1975, following the Jupiter encounter, the micrometer detector was turned off.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-InDepth_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-InDepth-3">[3]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<style Commons</div></div></div></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Saturn_encounter">Saturn encounter</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pioneer_11&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Saturn encounter">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p><i>Pioneer 11</i> passed by Saturn on September 1, 1979, at a distance of 21,000&#160;km from Saturn\'s cloud tops.\n</p><p>By this time <i><a href="/wiki/Voyager_1" title="Voyager 1">Voyager 1</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Voyager_2" title="Voyager 2">Voyager 2</a></i> had already passed Jupiter and were also en route to Saturn, so it was decided to target <i>Pioneer 11</i> to pass through the Saturn ring plane at the same position that the soon-to-come Voyager probes would use in order to test the route before the Voyagers arrived. If there were faint ring particles that could damage a probe in that area, mission planners felt it was better to learn about it via Pioneer. Thus, <i>Pioneer 11</i> was acting as a "pioneer" in a true sense of the word; if danger were detected, then the Voyager probes could be rerouted further away from the rings, but missing the opportunity to visit Uranus and Neptune in the process.\n</p><p><i>Pioneer 11</i> imaged and nearly collided with one of Saturn\'s small moons, passing at a distance of no more than 4,000 kilometers (2,500&#160;mi). The object was tentatively identified as <a href="/wiki/Epimetheus_(moon)" title="Epimetheus (moon)">Epimetheus</a>, a moon discovered the previous day from <i>Pioneer</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'</span>s imaging, and suspected from earlier observations by Earth-based telescopes. After the <a href="/wiki/Voyager_program" title="Voyager program">Voyager</a> flybys, it became known that there are two similarly-sized moons (Epimetheus and <a href="/wiki/Janus_(moon)" title="Janus (moon)">Janus</a>) in the same orbit, so there is some uncertainty about which one was the object of Pioneer\'s near-miss. <i>Pioneer 11</i> encountered Janus on September 1, 1979 at 14:52 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UTC" title="UTC">UTC</a> at a distance of 2500&#160;km and <a href="/wiki/Mimas_(moon)" title="Mimas (moon)">Mimas</a> at 16:20 UTC the same day at 103000&#160;km.\n</p><p>Besides Epimetheus, instruments located another previously undiscovered small moon and an additional ring, charted Saturn\'s magnetosphere and magnetic field and found its planet-size moon, <a href="/wiki/Titan_(moon)" title="Titan (moon)">Titan</a>, to be too cold for life. Hurtling underneath the ring plane, the probe sent back pictures of Saturn\'s rings. The rings, which normally seem bright when observed from Earth, appeared dark in the Pioneer pictures, and the dark gaps in the rings seen from Earth appeared as bright rings.\n</p>\n<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r874315073" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="mod-gallery mod-gallery-default mod-gallery-center"><div class="main"><div><ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional nochecker bordered-images" style="line-height:130%;">\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 210px"><div style="width: 210px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 205px;"><div style="margin:36px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:P11F81.jpg"><img alt="Pioneer 11 image of Saturn (image F81). class="footer"><div><div class="center" style="width:auto; margin-left:auto; margin-right:auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Commons-logo.svg"><img alt="" class="noviewer" data-file-height="1376" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="16" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4a/Commons-logo.svg/12px-Commons-logo.svg.png" width="12"/></a> Media related to <a class="extiw" href="https://commons.wikimedia.org/wiki/Category:Photos_of_Saturn_by_Pioneer_11" title="commons:Category:Photos of Saturn by Pioneer 11"><i>Pioneer 11</i> Saturn encounter</a> at Wikimedia Commons</div></div></div></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Interstellar_mission">Interstellar mission</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pioneer_11&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Interstellar mission">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>On February 25, 1990, <i>Pioneer 11</i> became the 4th man-made object to pass beyond the orbit of the planets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="NASA_ends_operations">NASA ends operations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pioneer_11&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: NASA ends operations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>By 1995, <i>Pioneer 11</i> could no longer power any of its detectors, so the decision was made to shut it down.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Farewell_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Farewell-29">[29]</a></sup> On September 29, 1995, NASA\'s <a href="/wiki/Ames_Research_Center" title="Ames Research Center">Ames Research Center</a>, responsible for managing the project, issued a press release that began, "After nearly 22 years of exploration out to the farthest reaches of the Solar System, one of the most durable and productive space missions in history will come to a close."  It indicated NASA would use its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deep_Space_Network" title="Deep Space Network">Deep Space Network</a> antennas to listen "once or twice a month" for the spacecraft\'s signal, until "some time in late 1996" when "its transmitter will fall silent altogether."  NASA Administrator <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Goldin" title="Daniel Goldin">Daniel Goldin</a> characterized <i>Pioneer 11</i> as "the little spacecraft that could, a venerable explorer that has taught us a great deal about the Solar System and, in the end, about our own innate drive to learn. <i>Pioneer 11</i> is what NASA is all about &#8211; exploration beyond the frontier."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ames_Release_95-163_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ames_Release_95-163-30">[30]</a></sup> Besides announcing the end of operations, the dispatch provided a historical list of <i>Pioneer 11</i> mission achievements. NASA terminated routine contact with the spacecraft on September 30, 1995, but continued to make contact for about 2 hours every 2 to 4 weeks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Farewell_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-Farewell-29">[29]</a></sup> Scientists received a few minutes of good engineering data on 24 November 1995 but then lost final contact once Earth permanently moved out of view of the spacecraft\'s antenna.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-InDepth_3-3"><a href="#cite_note-InDepth-3">[3]</a></sup> Its signal became too faint to hear in 2002.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Current_status">Current status</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pioneer_11&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Current status">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pioneer_speed_and_distance_from_Sun.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="649" data-file-width="847" decoding="async" height="169" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Pioneer_speed_and_distance_from_Sun.svg/220px-Pioneer_speed_and_distance_from_Sun.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Pioneer_speed_and_distance_from_Sun.svg/330px-Pioneer_speed_and_distance_from_Sun.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Pioneer_speed_and_distance_from_Sun.svg/440px-Pioneer_speed_and_distance_from_Sun.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pioneer_speed_and_distance_from_Sun.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>:Pioneer 10 and 11 speed and distance from the Sun</div></div></div>\n<p>On January 30, 2019, <i>Pioneer 11</i> was 100.84&#160;AU (1.5085<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">&#215;</span>10<sup>10</sup>&#160;km; 9.374<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">&#215;</span>10<sup>9</sup>&#160;mi) from the Earth and 100&#160;AU (1.5<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">&#215;</span>10<sup>10</sup>&#160;km; 9.3<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">&#215;</span>10<sup>9</sup>&#160;mi) from the Sun; and traveling at 11.241&#160;km/s (40,470&#160;km/h; 25,150&#160;mph) (relative to the Sun) and traveling outward at about 2.37 AU per year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> The spacecraft is heading in the direction of the constellation <a href="/wiki/Scutum_(constellation)" title="Scutum (constellation)">Scutum</a> near the current position (August 2017) <a href="/wiki/Right_ascension" title="Right ascension">RA</a> 18h 50m <a href="/wiki/Declination" title="Declination">dec</a> -8&#176; 39.5\' (<a href="/wiki/Epoch_(astronomy)#Julian_years_and_J2000" title="Epoch (astronomy)">J2000.0</a>) close to <a href="/wiki/Messier_26" title="Messier 26">Messier 26</a>.\n</p><p><i>Pioneer 11</i> has now been overtaken by the two Voyager probes, launched in 1977, and <i><a href="/wiki/Voyager_1" title="Voyager 1">Voyager 1</a></i> is now the most distant object built by humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Pioneer_anomaly">Pioneer anomaly</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pioneer_11&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Pioneer anomaly">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Pioneer_anomaly" title="Pioneer anomaly">Pioneer anomaly</a></div>\n<p>Analysis of the radio tracking data from the <i>Pioneer 10</i> and <i>11</i> spacecraft at distances between 20&#8211;70 AU from the Sun has consistently indicated the presence of a small but anomalous <a href="/wiki/Doppler_effect" title="Doppler effect">Doppler</a> frequency drift. The drift can be interpreted as due to a constant acceleration of <span class="nowrap">(8.74 &#177; 1.33) &#215; 10<sup>&#8722;10</sup> m/s<sup>2</sup></span> directed towards the Sun. Although it is suspected that there is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Systematic_bias" title="Systematic bias">systematic origin</a> to the effect, none was found. As a result, there is sustained interest in the nature of this so-called "<a href="/wiki/Pioneer_anomaly" title="Pioneer anomaly">Pioneer anomaly</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-britt20041018_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-britt20041018-34">[34]</a></sup> Extended analysis of mission data by Slava Turyshev and colleagues has determined the source of the anomaly to be asymmetric thermal radiation and the resulting thermal recoil force acting on the face of the Pioneers away from the Sun,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ps00003459_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-ps00003459-35">[35]</a></sup> and in July 2012 the group of researchers published their results in the <i><a href="/wiki/Physical_Review_Letters" title="Physical Review Letters">Physical Review Letters</a></i> scientific journal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Pioneer_plaque">Pioneer plaque</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pioneer_11&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Pioneer plaque">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Pioneer_plaque" title="Pioneer plaque">Pioneer plaque</a></div>\n<p><i>Pioneer 10</i> and <i>11</i> both carry a gold-anodized aluminum <a href="/wiki/Commemorative_plaque" title="Commemorative plaque">plaque</a> in the event that either spacecraft is ever found by intelligent lifeforms from other planetary systems. The plaques feature the nude figures of a human male and female along with several symbols that are designed to provide information about the origin of the spacecraft.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sagan1972_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sagan1972-37">[37]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Commemoration">Commemoration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pioneer_11&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Commemoration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>In 1991, <i>Pioneer 11</i> was honored on one of 10 United States Postage Service stamps commemorating unmanned spacecraft exploring each of the then nine planets and the Moon. <i>Pioneer 11</i> was the spacecraft featured with Jupiter. Pluto was listed as "Not yet explored".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Gallery">Gallery</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pioneer_11&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Gallery">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303569">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output
